Plinky 12 panel-authoring context for coding agents.

This file is generated from the public panel headers and curated example panels.
Treat the headers as the authoritative API surface. Use the examples as style and usage guides.

Guidance:
- Prefer the public panel API in `blocks.h`, `blocks_ll.h`, `synth.h`, and `save_and_load.h`.
- Write straightforward panel code derived from `panel_t`.
- Avoid inventing private helpers or including internal headers unless the public API clearly does not expose what you need.
- Prefer static state inside the panel over dynamic allocation.
- Follow the example panels for style, lifecycle, and UI patterns.
- There is no need to #include the headers, that is done for you. In fact, #include is banned from the panel code.

Generated artifacts:
- Markdown docs: docs/ide_api/ide_api.md
- Concatenated header bundle: docs/ide_api/plinky_library.h
- Main LLM context: docs/ide_api/llm.txt

## Top-Down Panel Authoring Guide

When turning a vague panel idea into working code, choose the highest-level public helper that matches the job before building
from raw pads:

  - Start from `panel_t`. Keep persistent state as panel members, override only the lifecycle hooks you need, and keep
    `on_ui(...)`, `on_sequence(...)`, and `on_dsp(...)` responsibilities separate.
  - For playable note areas, prefer `play_surface_t::do_play_surface(...)` plus `voice_allocator_t` and `play_synth(...)` or
    the level-triggered MIDI helpers. This gives you Plinky-style pressure, strings, scale mapping, and voice tracking.
  - For sequencers and arpeggiators, use `clock_divider_t` to turn transport phase into musical edges, then use `playhead_t`
    for step order, loop bounds, shuffle, ping-pong, and arp traversal.
  - For synth editing, start with `preset_pages_t`, `synth_xy_block(...)`, `synth_param_sliders_block(...)`, and
    `synth_flags_button(...)`. Drop to individual sliders only when the panel needs a custom layout.
  - For preset browsing or full panel save/load pages, prefer `preset_pages_t`, `panel_page_t`, and `file_picker_t` instead of
    recreating the picker grid, preview, delete, staged load, and save-button rules.
  - For simple controls, use `button(...)`, `shift_button(...)`, `checkbox_button(...)`, `radio_buttons(...)`, `slider_t`,
    `xy_pad_t`, and the `last_widget_*()` accessors.
  - For panel-local preferences such as display hue or audio source, implement `on_serialise_settings(...)`, expose the page
    with `get_num_panel_settings_pages()`, and render it with `draw_system_style_settings_page(...)` or its bool/enum variants.
  - For durable song or pattern state, implement `on_serialise(...)` with the field macros from `save_and_load.h`. Rebuild
    derived tables after reads, and use `serialise_psram_binary_footer(...)` only for large PSRAM-backed buffers.
  - For custom visual/touch behaviours, use the raw LED and touch helpers in `blocks_ll.h` directly, but keep this below the
    higher-level widget/surface layer unless the panel concept really needs custom interaction.
  - For custom audio, override `on_dsp(...)`. Return `false` after filling `mix_buffers_out` when you want Plinky's standard
    FX/output chain, or return `true` after writing final stereo samples directly to `audiobuf_out`.

### Pages And Navigation

The right physical buttons cycle through pages. Panel pages live below page 0 on the taller logical grid:

  - page 0 is visible at logical `y = 0`
  - page 1 is at `y = 16`
  - page 2 is at `y = 32`, and so on

Advertise ordinary panel pages by overriding `get_num_pages()`. A panel with `get_num_pages() == 2` should draw its second page
when `scroll_y` places that page in view, usually by passing a `y` offset such as `16` to a page helper.

Panel-owned settings pages live above page 0 and are inserted before the global system pages:

  - panel settings page 0 is at `y = -16`
  - panel settings page 1 is at `y = -32`

Advertise these by overriding `get_num_panel_settings_pages()`. Render them with `draw_system_style_settings_page(...)`,
`draw_system_style_bool_settings_page(...)`, or `draw_system_style_enum_settings_page(...)`. This matches the built-in page
navigation users expect from the flagship panels.

### Runtime Hooks

Use `on_ui(...)` to paint the current 16x16 view and emit UI controls. It normally runs around 250 fps on the main thread of
core 0, but it can pause during SD-card work or other foreground tasks, so it is the wrong place for timing-critical musical state.

Use `on_sequence(...)` for musical decisions: advancing playheads, sequencing notes, refreshing voice allocations, declaring MIDI
notes, and updating synth note state. It runs roughly 500-1000 fps in an interrupt context on core 0 and keeps running while the
foreground thread is blocked on storage. Keep it short, deterministic, and free of SD-card or large-memory work.

Use `on_dsp(...)` for audio. It runs on core 1 and must prepare one 64-sample block. The default implementation renders the
built-in synth and returns `false`, so the normal Plinky mix and FX chain runs after it. Custom panels should usually fill
`mix_buffers_out->dry`, `mix_buffers_out->delaysend`, and `mix_buffers_out->reverbsend`, then return `false`. Return `true` only
after writing final interleaved stereo samples directly to `audiobuf_out`, which bypasses the built-in FX/output chain for that
block.

Other common overrides:

  - `setup_default_panel_state()` builds the default song/panel state after settings have been loaded.
  - `on_click(...)` handles the four physical side buttons; call `panel_t::on_click(...)` for bits the system should keep owning.
  - `on_touch(...)` is a low-level pad edge callback; most panels use the per-frame touch/widget helpers instead.
  - `on_midi(...)` handles incoming packed MIDI messages; pair it with `voice_allocator_t` or level-triggered MIDI helpers.
  - `pump_usb_serial()` is for panels acting as USB CDC serial devices.
  - `on_dsp_core0_final_mix_unsafe(...)`, `on_dsp_param_override(...)`, and `on_dsp_mix_state_override(...)` are specialised DSP
    hooks. The core0 final-mix hook is for tiny bounded stock-panel work only.
  - `on_prepare_staged_load(...)`, `on_commit_staged_load_audio_thread(...)`, and `on_load_finished()` are staged-load hooks.
  - `get_version()` and `get_settings_version()` version saved song state and panel settings separately.
  - `get_num_tracks()` and `get_track_name(...)` describe panel tracks to host/control surfaces.

### Persistence Lifetimes

`on_serialise(...)` is for song-like state chosen by the user: patterns, clip contents, panel page state, preset choices, and any
mix/preset data that should travel with a saved panel slot. `on_serialise_settings(...)` is for panel-local preferences that should
come back automatically on boot or reset, such as a display hue, MIDI routing preference, or audio input source. Settings are copied
from the live panel into staged loads so loading a song does not silently overwrite the user's durable preferences.

Constructors should leave cheap, valid defaults only. Put user-facing defaults in `setup_default_panel_state()`, and rebuild
derived tables after `on_serialise(...)` reads fields back.

### Coding Constraints

Stack space is scarce. Keep ordinary stack locals small; avoid objects larger than about 128 bytes and treat 256 bytes as a hard
upper bound unless you know exactly which context you are in. Store larger persistent state as members of your panel object, but
the entire panel object must fit in the 128 KB panel arena.

Do not use heap allocation. There is no useful heap for panel code: avoid `new`, `malloc`, `free`, growing containers, and APIs
that allocate behind your back. If you need brief foreground scratch space for a large temporary, use the core-0 temp allocator
helpers from `blocks_ll.h`.

There is a second 128 KB panel arena exposed as shadow state. It is useful for optional undo/redo snapshots or other temporary
same-panel copies, but the save/load and preset systems also use it. It can disappear or be overwritten; always use the generation
number returned by `get_panel_shadow_state(...)` / `get_panel_shadow_state_generation()` and degrade gracefully when it is unavailable.

The larger stock panels follow this pattern: pick one primary interaction surface, add conventional transport controls near the
bottom right, expose preset or panel save/load via the ready-made pages, and only then fill in bespoke controls. The examples are
small slices of that style: `paint.cpp` for direct LED/touch interaction, `mics.cpp` for DSP analysis and display, `grid.cpp` for
USB serial plus a panel settings page, and `worm.cpp` for an advanced PSRAM audio sampler.


==== Public API Headers ====

===== blocks.h =====

typedef struct voice_params voice_params;
extern "C" int find_best_multisample_for_note(const synth_preset_t *preset, int note);

/*
# blocks.h

`blocks.h` is the main panel-authoring API.

It sits on top of [`blocks_ll.h`](#doc-blocks-ll-h), which exposes the raw hardware view. This file adds the layer most panel
authors actually want: panel lifecycle hooks, transport helpers, playheads, semi-immediate-mode widgets, save/load UI, and access to
the built-in synth and preset system.

The core idea is simple: a panel is a class derived from `panel_t`, and that class owns all of the panel's state and
behaviour.

Plinky itself is a 16x16 RGB LED grid where every pad also senses pressure. It has stereo audio in/out, TRS MIDI in and two TRS
MIDI outs, USB MIDI, an SD card for presets and panel state, plus CV/clock/reset I/O. The runtime handles scanning the hardware,
updating LEDs, and routing the built-in synth.

Recommended style:

  - write straightforward "C / C++" code
  - do not use dynamic allocation (`malloc`, `free`, `new`, growing containers)
  - instead, declare 'worst-case' state directly inside your panel
  - keep memory use predictable

The panel instance is the whole panel. The hard size limit for your panel is 128 KB.
Size your state accordingly. The target platform is arm cortex m33, RP2350 MCU.

The runtime calls into your panel from three main execution contexts:

  - `on_ui(...)` for reading touches and drawing LEDs (main core, can stall during SD access)
  - `on_sequence(...)` for timing-sensitive musical logic (high-priority timer / interrupt, should stay light)
  - `on_dsp(...)` for audio generation (core 1, must finish one 64-sample block)

Saving and loading panel state is system-managed. Your panel chooses what is persistent by implementing `on_serialise(...)` using
the helpers in [`save_and_load.h`](#saveandloadh).

The system manages a set of 'presets' (think, synth sounds) on `panel_t` itself. These can reference sample data,
which is loaded and managed for you, but can be read easily via a macro READ_SAMPLE (documented below).
*/

/*
## Choosing The Right Layer

Most panels become more Plinky-like when they start from the largest matching helper and only drop down a layer for the parts that
are genuinely custom.

Useful first choices:

  - playable note or string surface: `play_surface_t::do_play_surface(...)`
  - step sequencer or arpeggiator: `clock_divider_t` plus `playhead_t`
  - synth preset editor: `preset_pages_t`, `synth_xy_block(...)`, `synth_param_sliders_block(...)`, and `synth_flags_button(...)`
  - preset browser: `preset_pages_t::saveload(...)` or `file_picker_t::preset_picker(...)`
  - panel save/load page: `panel_page_t::saveload(...)`
  - ordinary controls: `button(...)`, `shift_button(...)`, `checkbox_button(...)`, `slider_t`, and `xy_pad_t`
  - panel preferences: `on_serialise_settings(...)` plus a page from `draw_system_style_settings_page(...)`
  - custom raw grid behaviour: LED/touch helpers from `blocks_ll.h`
  - custom audio: override `on_dsp(...)`; return `false` to run Plinky's standard FX/output chain on `mix_buffers_out`, or
    return `true` after writing final stereo samples directly to `audiobuf_out`

The bigger stock panels use the same public building blocks heavily. A typical structure is one main interaction surface, a
bottom-row transport/control convention, optional synth-edit or save/load pages, and a small number of custom controls around the
specific musical idea.
*/

/*
## Pages And Physical Navigation

Plinky's visible grid is one 16x16 window onto a taller logical surface. The right physical buttons cycle through pages.

Ordinary panel pages live below page 0:

  - page 0 is visible at logical `y = 0`
  - page 1 is at `y = 16`
  - page 2 is at `y = 32`, and so on

Override `get_num_pages()` to advertise these pages. For example, returning `2` gives the panel page 0 plus one extra page at
`y = 16`; whole-page helpers such as `panel_page_t::saveload(...)` and `preset_pages_t` take a `y` argument so you can place
them at that logical offset.

Panels can also advertise panel-owned settings pages above page 0 by overriding `get_num_panel_settings_pages()`. These pages are
inserted before the global system pages:

  - panel settings page 0 is at `y = -16`
  - panel settings page 1 is at `y = -32`

Settings pages are intended to use the compact system-style renderers from `blocks_ll.h`:
`draw_system_style_settings_page(...)`, `draw_system_style_bool_settings_page(...)`, or
`draw_system_style_enum_settings_page(...)`. This follows the navigation model users already learn from the built-in pages and the
larger stock panels.
*/

/*
## Lifecycle Hooks And Execution Contexts

The runtime calls a panel from several contexts. Keeping each kind of work in the right hook is the main difference between a panel
that merely compiles and one that feels solid on hardware.

`on_ui(...)` paints the current 16x16 view and emits UI controls such as buttons, sliders, XY pads, and file pickers. It normally
runs around 250 fps on the main thread of core 0. Because it is foreground work, it can pause during SD-card access and other
longer system tasks. Use it for display, touch interpretation, widget editing, and non-timing-critical state changes.

`on_sequence(...)` is for musical decisions: advancing clock dividers and playheads, sequencing notes, refreshing voice allocator
claims, declaring MIDI notes, updating synth note state, and publishing current harmonic state. It runs roughly 500-1000 fps in an
interrupt context on core 0 and keeps running while the foreground thread is blocked on storage. Keep it short, deterministic, and
free of SD-card work or large memory scans.

`on_dsp(...)` runs on core 1 and owns one 64-sample audio block. The default implementation renders Plinky's built-in synth voices
into `mix_buffers_out` and returns `false`, which tells the runtime to run the standard delay/reverb/final output chain. Custom
panels normally also return `false` after filling `mix_buffers_out->dry`, `mix_buffers_out->delaysend`, and
`mix_buffers_out->reverbsend`. Return `true` only when your panel has written final interleaved stereo `int16_t` samples directly
to `audiobuf_out` and wants to bypass the built-in FX/output chain for that block.

`mix_buffers_out` is cleared before `on_dsp(...)` is called. It currently points at the global `mix_buffers`, so existing helpers
that write `mix_buffers` still work, but new panel code should prefer the parameter because it documents the handoff explicitly.
`worm.cpp` is a good advanced example: it replaces the usual synth voice generation with a PSRAM granular sampler, but still
reuses the standard mix/FX path by returning `false`.

The other overrides are narrower:

  - `setup_default_panel_state()` builds the default user-facing song/panel after settings have loaded
  - `on_click(...)` handles the four physical side buttons; pass through bits the system should keep owning
  - `on_touch(...)` is a low-level per-pad edge callback; most panels use widget/touch helpers instead
  - `on_midi(...)` handles incoming packed MIDI messages
  - `pump_usb_serial()` is for panels acting as USB CDC serial devices
  - `on_dsp_core0_final_mix_unsafe(...)` is an internal/advanced stock-panel hook that runs on core 0 from the high-priority
    audio-footer doorbell; keep it tiny, bounded, and non-blocking
  - `on_dsp_param_override(...)` and `on_dsp_mix_state_override(...)` are specialised built-in-synth DSP hooks
  - `on_prepare_staged_load(...)`, `on_commit_staged_load_audio_thread(...)`, and `on_load_finished()` customise staged loads
  - `get_version()` and `get_settings_version()` version saved song state and panel settings separately
  - `get_num_tracks()` and `get_track_name(...)` describe panel tracks to host/control surfaces
*/

/*
## Persistence Lifetimes

There are two panel-owned persistence tiers:

  - `on_serialise(...)` is for song-like state chosen by the user: patterns, clips, panel page state, preset choices, and any
    mix/preset data that should travel with a saved panel slot
  - `on_serialise_settings(...)` is for panel-local preferences that should come back automatically on boot or reset, such as a
    display hue, MIDI routing preference, or audio input source

Settings are copied from the live panel into staged loads before saved-song data is read, so loading a song does not silently
overwrite the user's durable preferences. Constructors should leave cheap, valid defaults only. Put user-facing defaults in
`setup_default_panel_state()`, and rebuild derived tables after `on_serialise(...)` reads fields back.
*/

/*
## Coding Constraints

Stack space is scarce. Keep ordinary stack locals small; avoid objects larger than about 128 bytes and treat 256 bytes as a hard
upper bound unless you know exactly which context you are in. Store larger persistent state as members of your panel object, but
the entire panel object must fit in the 128 KB panel arena.

Do not use heap allocation. There is no useful heap for panel code: avoid `new`, `malloc`, `free`, growing containers, and APIs
that allocate behind your back. If you need brief foreground scratch space for a large temporary, use the core-0 temp allocator
helpers from `blocks_ll.h`.

There is a second 128 KB panel arena exposed as shadow state. It is useful for optional undo/redo snapshots or other temporary
same-panel copies, but the save/load and preset systems also use it. It can disappear or be overwritten; always use the generation
number returned by `get_panel_shadow_state(...)` / `get_panel_shadow_state_generation()` and degrade gracefully when it is
unavailable.
*/

/*
## Panel Lifecycle

Derive from `panel_t` to create a panel. The runtime constructs your panel once, then repeatedly calls the hooks below.
Each hook is for a different kind of work, so keeping responsibilities separated makes panels easier to reason about and keeps
timing predictable.
*/
struct serialiser_t;
extern "C" void default_panel_button_click(uint8_t button_mask);
void synth_note_up_all(void);
bool serialise(serialiser_t &s, lfo_recording_t &o);
/*
PSRAM binary footer codecs:

  - `PSRAM_BINARY_FOOTER_CODEC_RAW_BINARY` writes the selected byte range directly, without compression.
  - `PSRAM_BINARY_FOOTER_CODEC_ZERO_RLE` is lossless zero-run-length compression. It is useful when the buffer contains lots of
    exact zeroes, such as sparse sample or undo buffers.
  - `PSRAM_BINARY_FOOTER_CODEC_LOSSY_STEREO_8BIT` is a fast ADPCM-style lossy stereo codec. It is relatively high quality and
    gives a fixed 2:1 compression ratio, making it a good fit for large audio buffers where save speed matters.
*/
enum psram_binary_footer_codec_t {
  PSRAM_BINARY_FOOTER_CODEC_RAW_BINARY,
  PSRAM_BINARY_FOOTER_CODEC_ZERO_RLE,
  PSRAM_BINARY_FOOTER_CODEC_LOSSY_STEREO_8BIT,
};
/*
Register a PSRAM byte range to be appended to panel save JSON as a binary footer.

Call this after `OBJECT_END(s)` in `on_serialise(...)`. The default range is all panel-visible PSRAM. The codec argument chooses
the save codec only; loads use the codec tag stored in the file.
*/
bool serialise_psram_binary_footer(serialiser_t &s, psram_binary_footer_codec_t codec = PSRAM_BINARY_FOOTER_CODEC_RAW_BINARY,
                                   uint32_t psram_offset_bytes = 0, uint32_t byte_count = UINT32_MAX);
struct panel_t {
  /* Every panel instance owns its synth preset bank, including the mix slot.
  A staged panel copy therefore carries staged presets automatically. */
  synth_preset_t synth_presets[NUM_PRESETS_INCLUDING_MIX];
  lfo_recording_t lfo_recordings[MODULATION_SOURCE_LAST];
  uint32_t modulation_target_mask; // runtime-only aggregate of synth preset modulation targets.
  uint8_t modulation_source_mask;  // runtime-only aggregate of synth preset modulation sources.
  char song_name[17];              // user-facing save/controller name, matching preset name length.

  virtual bool on_prepare_staged_load(panel_t *staged_state, size_t panel_memory_size) {
    (void)staged_state;
    (void)panel_memory_size;
    return true;
  } // core0: optional hook after the system constructs a clean inactive staged instance, before deserialising into it.
  virtual void on_commit_staged_load_audio_thread(panel_t *loaded_state, size_t panel_memory_size) {
    synth_note_up_all();
    memcpy((void *)this, (const void *)loaded_state, panel_memory_size);
    refresh_modulation_masks();
  } // core1: apply the complete staged copy at the audio-safe commit point. default is memcpy.
  virtual void on_load_finished(void) {}
  // core0 main thread: the staged load is now live, and panel on_midi/on_sequence remain suppressed until this hook returns.
  virtual int get_version(void) { return 1; }      // saved-state schema version. use this to evolve old files safely.
  virtual int get_num_pages(void) { return 1; }    // number of user pages below page 0.
  virtual int get_num_panel_settings_pages(void) { return 0; } // panel-owned settings pages above page 0, before system pages.
  /*
  Set up the panel's default user-facing state.

  Panel construction should leave a clean, valid, side-effect-free instance: no SD reads, no preset loads, no MIDI/global state
  publication. Put cheap built-in defaults there, including defaults for any fields owned by `on_serialise_settings(...)`.
  `setup_default_panel_state()` is the place to build the default song/pattern/preset state used at first startup and by the
  blank-panel load action.

  The system auto-loads panel settings from SD before calling `setup_default_panel_state()` at boot. It tries both filename
  styles in this order:

    1. shared panel settings without a serial number
    2. per-device panel settings with the board serial number

  The serial-numbered file is treated as more specific, so if both exist its values win because it loads second.

  Normal saved-song loads and blank-panel loads do not read settings from SD. The system copies the live panel's declared settings
  into the staged panel in memory before deserialising the song or building the default state.
  */
  virtual void setup_default_panel_state();
  void refresh_modulation_masks();
  virtual void on_sequence(int delta_time_us) {}; // timing-critical musical logic. high-priority timer / irq context.
  virtual void on_ui(int delta_time_us) {};       // foreground UI: read touch, emit widgets, and draw LEDs.
  virtual void on_click(uint8_t button_mask) {
    default_panel_button_click(button_mask);
  }; // physical clicky buttons; left bits may autorepeat.
  virtual void on_midi(uint32_t midimsg) {};        // incoming MIDI callback as a packed 32-bit message. interrupt context.
  virtual void on_touch(int x, int y, int down) {}; // optional per-pad edge callback for touch transitions.
  virtual void pump_usb_serial() {};                // optional USB CDC serial pump; use usb_serial_* helpers from blocks_ll.h.
  virtual bool on_serialise(serialiser_t &s, int version); // save/load durable panel state via the helpers in save_and_load.h.
  /*
  Panel settings are the "middle tier" of persistence:

    - panel save/load slots are for song-like state chosen by the user
    - panel settings are for durable preferences owned by one panel
    - system config is for whole-device settings such as calibration

  Implement `on_serialise_settings(...)` when your panel has durable settings that are not part of a song. The format uses the
  same fixed-shape JSON helpers as normal panel save/load. A typical settings object contains a few booleans or small integers.

  Keep this separate from `on_serialise(...)` on purpose:

    - `on_serialise(...)` should describe musical state that belongs in a save slot
    - `on_serialise_settings(...)` should describe panel-local preferences that should come back automatically on reset/boot

  `get_settings_version()` is the schema version for that settings file only.
  */
  virtual int get_settings_version(void) { return 1; }
  virtual bool on_serialise_settings(serialiser_t &s, int version);
  virtual void on_dsp_param_override(int preset_idx, int voice_idx, voice_params *params) {
    (void)preset_idx;
    (void)voice_idx;
    (void)params;
  }
  virtual void on_dsp_mix_state_override(mix_state_t *mix) {
    (void)mix;
  }
  /*
  Core 1 audio callback. Return false after filling mix_buffers_out for Plinky's built-in FX/output chain. Return true after
  writing final interleaved stereo int16 samples directly to audiobuf_out.
  */
  virtual bool on_dsp(const int16_t *audiobuf_in, int16_t *audiobuf_out, mix_buffers_t *mix_buffers_out);
  /*
  Advanced/internal: runs on core 0 from the high-priority audio-footer doorbell inside do_fx(). Use only for tiny, bounded final
  dry-block tweaks in stock panels. User panels should prefer on_dsp(), which runs on core 1.
  */
  virtual void on_dsp_core0_final_mix_unsafe(const int16_t *audiobuf_in, int *dry_stereo);
  virtual int get_num_tracks(void) { return 8; }
  virtual const char *get_track_name(int i, char buf[16]) {
    if (!buf) return NULL;
    snprintf(buf, 16, "Track %d", (i & 7) + 1);
    return buf;
  }
  virtual ~panel_t(void) {};

  bool load_preset_from_filename_with_category(int new_preset_idx, const char *bank_category, const char *bank_filename,
                                               int preset_in_bank_idx, int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0,
                                               bool dont_touch_xy = false,
                                               const synth_preset_t **loaded_preset_out = nullptr);
  bool load_panel_from_json(const char *json);
  bool load_default_panel_state_from_json(const char *json);
  /*
  Load or save this panel's settings JSON on the SD card.

  These helpers call your `on_serialise_settings(...)` implementation and store the result in a single file owned by the panel.
  They do not use the song save-slot UI and they do not touch global system config.

  `include_serial_number` controls whether the filename is device-specific:

    - `false`: one shared settings file for this panel name
    - `true`: include the board serial in the filename so settings stay per-device

  The helpers mount the SD card if needed and fail cleanly if storage is unavailable or the file does not exist.
  */
  bool load_settings_from_sd(bool include_serial_number);
  bool save_settings_to_sd(bool include_serial_number);
};

/*
Returns true while a staged panel load is complete and waiting for a commit request.
Panels that want musical-boundary loads normally stage the load from `on_ui(...)`, then poll this from `on_sequence(...)` and call
`file_picker_t::request_panel_load_finalise()` at a barline, stop point, or other safe musical moment.
*/
bool is_panel_load_staged(void);

/*
## Shadow Panel State

The runtime keeps two 128 KB panel-memory arenas:

  - the live panel, which owns the currently running `panel_t`
  - the shadow panel, normally used as the staged target for panel loads, panel saves, blank-panel loads, and preset staging

`get_panel_shadow_state(...)` exposes that shadow panel to the currently running panel only while the runtime is not already using it.
This is intended for large, short-lived same-panel snapshots that would be too expensive to keep inside the live panel object, such
as an undo copy of a clip or pattern range. The returned pointer is a `panel_t *`; panel code may cast it to its own concrete type
after checking for `NULL`.

The function returns `NULL` when the shadow state is unavailable, including when there is no active panel, a panel load/finalise is
staged or pending, serialisation is in progress, or preset installs are staged/pending. Callers must treat `NULL` as "scratch
unavailable" and continue without that optional feature.

The shadow state is not durable and may be overwritten by the next save, load, blank-panel load, preset load, or panel restart. If
you keep metadata that points into or describes data copied into the shadow panel, store the generation returned through
`generation_out`. Before using the shadow data later, call `get_panel_shadow_state(...)` again and require both a non-`NULL` pointer
and the same generation. `get_panel_shadow_state_generation()` is provided for code that only needs to compare or invalidate metadata.

Do not use this as shared state between panels, saved state, or audio/sequence-thread state. Use it from foreground/UI code for
optional scratch features only.
*/
panel_t *get_panel_shadow_state(uint32_t *generation_out = nullptr);
uint32_t get_panel_shadow_state_generation(void);

/*
## Paged UI Model

The visible 16x16 grid is one window onto a taller logical surface. Page 0 is the first page of your panel. Additional panel
pages live "below" it in the positive Y direction, and `get_num_pages()` tells the system how many of those user pages exist.

System pages live "above" page 0. In normal use the right physical buttons change page, while the left pair nudge BPM (or adjust
the current system value while a system page is showing). Panels can override `on_click(...)` to remap those buttons while still
calling the default handler for any bits they want the system to keep owning. Panels like `toadstep` use page 1 for save/load UI.

Panels can reserve their own pages above page 0 by overriding `get_num_panel_settings_pages()`. These pages come before the global system
settings pages, so the first right-up click can show a panel-local settings page while the next click continues to Sensitivity.
Use `draw_system_style_settings_page(...)` from `blocks_ll.h` for a compact built-in settings page renderer, or draw directly with
`scroll_y` as usual.
*/

/*
## Global Transport

Panels commonly use the bottom-right corner as transport controls:

  - `(15, 15)` play / arm
  - `(14, 15)` stop
  - `(13, 15)` clear or reset
  - `(12, 15)` record

That layout is a convention, not a requirement, but users will often expect it. The low-level transport state and clock source API
now live in [`blocks_ll.h`](#doc-blocks-ll-h); the helpers below build sequencer behaviour on top of that shared transport.
*/
extern bool record_state;

static inline bool is_recording(void) { return record_state; }
static inline void stop_recording(void) { record_state = false; }
static inline void start_recording(void) { record_state = true; }

/*
### UI/Sequence Lock Guard

`on_ui(...)` normally should not edit state that `on_sequence(...)` is walking, because the sequence callback can interrupt the
UI code midway through that mutation. If you do need to perform a small synchronous edit from `on_ui(...)`, wrap it in
`on_sequence_lock_guard_t`.

Typical example:

  - `on_ui(...)` deletes or retimes packed note data in response to a touch
  - `on_sequence(...)` scans that same packed note data for playback
  - the guard keeps `on_sequence(...)` out until the edit is complete

Keep locked regions short and straightforward.
*/
extern "C" void begin_on_sequence_lock(void);
extern "C" void end_on_sequence_lock(void);
struct on_sequence_lock_guard_t {
  on_sequence_lock_guard_t() { begin_on_sequence_lock(); }
  ~on_sequence_lock_guard_t() { end_on_sequence_lock(); }
};

/*
## Sequencer Helpers

The usual layering is:

  1. the global transport produces clock
  2. a `clock_divider_t` turns that into a musically useful subdivision
  3. a `playhead_t` advances through steps when the divider says it is time

These helpers are designed to make step sequencers, arpeggiators, and clocked modulation feel consistent across panels.
*/
static inline bool sequencer_should_advance_playhead(void) { // helper for sequencers
  /* `clock_divider_t` emits a synthetic edge when transport starts so panels can trigger the current step immediately.
  `playhead_t` should not advance on that same edge; the next real divider edge advances it. */
  if (!is_transport_playing()) return false;
  if (has_transport_just_started()) return false;
  if (has_sequencer_just_loaded()) return false;
  if (has_transport_just_seeked()) return false;
  return true;
}

int64_t warp_clock(int64_t clock, float amount, const int *shuffle_times, int num_shuffle_times);
int64_t warp_clock_swing(int64_t clock, float amount);                // warps a clock by a 16th note swing amount
int64_t warp_clock_stolper(int pattern, int64_t clock, float amount); // warp a clock by one of the 7 stolper patterns

static inline int euclid_rhythm(int stepidx, int numset, int numsteps = 8, int rot = 0) {
  if (numsteps < 1 || numset < 1) return 0;
  stepidx = ((stepidx - rot) % numsteps) + numsteps;
  return ((stepidx * numset) % numsteps) < numset;
}

/* ### `clock_divider_t`

A clock divider simply provides a way to 'latch' a numerator and denominator when a chosen clock boundary is crossed.
Calling update with (optionally) new divider settings will set them when needed.
The same update policy also chooses the grid used to rebase `clock_base`; divider phase is computed relative to that base.
`UPDATE_DIV_NOW` latches immediately without moving an already-established `clock_base`.
`UPDATE_DIV_NOW_AND_SNAP_CLOCK_BASE_TO_QUARTER_NOTE` latches immediately and keeps the base on the quarter-note grid, so it
avoids tiny sub-frame phase offsets when latching near a global clock boundary.
Its main update function returns how many divider edges were crossed since the previous call.
This is based on wrap detection of a subdivided 16-bit phase, so if the transport phase jumps
forward across multiple divider boundaries you get that crossed-edge count directly, unless the jump was an explicit transport
seek. Seeks rebase the divider and report `0` crossed edges so panels can decide whether to emit the current step.
Two special cases remain: on `has_transport_just_started()`, update returns at least `1` so sequencers can get a start edge
even when transport begins exactly on a divider boundary. On `has_sequencer_just_loaded()`, update returns exactly `1` so
sequencers get a first load edge without catching up through saved or stale divider history.

Passing `freerunning=true` makes the divider follow the free-running quarter-note phase instead of the transport phase. That mode
keeps tempo sync, continues while transport is stopped, and stays phase-locked to the transport whenever the transport is playing.
By default it uses the full 32-bit free-running accumulator. If you pass a wrapping q16 phase yourself, use `PLEASE_UNWRAP_CLOCK`
so the divider extends it using its previous phase.
*/
enum divider_update_policy_t : uint8_t {
  UPDATE_DIV_NOW,
  UPDATE_DIV_NOW_AND_SNAP_CLOCK_BASE_TO_QUARTER_NOTE,
  UPDATE_DIV_ON_QUARTER_NOTE,
  UPDATE_DIV_ON_HALF_NOTE,
  UPDATE_DIV_ON_BAR,
};

static constexpr uint32_t CLOCK_DIVIDER_SIXTEENTH_NOTE_Q16 = 16384u;
static constexpr uint32_t CLOCK_DIVIDER_QUARTER_NOTE_Q16 = 65536u;

#define DONT_UNWRAP_CLOCK 0
#define PLEASE_UNWRAP_CLOCK 1

struct clock_divider_t {
  int numerator, denominator;
  int64_t clock_base; // local origin for phase calculation; some update policies align it to a musical grid.
  uint16_t phase;     // where we are within the current step
  int update(int64_t clock_phase = -1, int requested_numerator = -1, int requested_denominator = -1,
             divider_update_policy_t update_when = UPDATE_DIV_ON_HALF_NOTE, bool freerunning = false,
             bool unwrap_clock = DONT_UNWRAP_CLOCK);
  void reset(int64_t clock_phase = 0, uint32_t grid_q16 = CLOCK_DIVIDER_QUARTER_NOTE_Q16,
             int requested_numerator = -1, int requested_denominator = -1);
  void set_clock_base(int64_t clock_phase, uint32_t grid_q16 = CLOCK_DIVIDER_QUARTER_NOTE_Q16);
  uint32_t get_color_for_ui(uint32_t base_color); // returns a pulsing colour based on the phase.
  uint16_t phase_q16(void) const { return phase; }
  uint8_t phase_q7(void) const { return (uint8_t)(phase >> 9); }
  int64_t last_clock_phase(void) const { return _last_clock_phase; }
  uint32_t step_index(void) const {
    int n = numerator < 1 ? 1 : numerator;
    int d = denominator < 1 ? 1 : denominator;
    int64_t scaled = (_last_clock_phase - clock_base) * (int64_t)n;
    scaled = scaled >= 0 ? scaled / d : -(((-scaled) + d - 1) / d);
    int64_t step = scaled >= 0 ? scaled / CLOCK_DIVIDER_QUARTER_NOTE_Q16
                               : -(((-scaled) + CLOCK_DIVIDER_QUARTER_NOTE_Q16 - 1) / CLOCK_DIVIDER_QUARTER_NOTE_Q16);
    return (uint32_t)step;
  }

  // internal
  int64_t _last_clock_phase; // clock phase used by the most recent update; prefer the accessors above.
};

/* ### `playhead_t`

A `playhead_t` provides a simple helper for any kind of step sequencer.
it has a position, and a range of steps (from and to) within which it will loop.
it is intended to be combined with a clock divider, and you pass `clock_divider_t::update() > 0`
to the playhead's update() function.
note that the order and end points are latched on the next step of the clock divider.
the playhead also provides a shuffle function, which will shuffle the steps in a fixed random order.
to change the order on each loop, change shuffle_seed whenever update returns true.
alternatively, use RANDOM_RESEED to automatically reseed on every loop.
it also provides accessors to check if we are on the first or last step of the current loop.
Use `get_position_with_clock_divider(...)` when you need the current step plus the q8 fractional offset from a clock divider.
*/
enum playorder_t : uint8_t {
  FORWARD,
  REVERSE,
  PINGPONG,
  PINGPONG_INCLUSIVE,
  RANDOM,
  RANDOM_RESEED,
  LAST,
};

/*
### Arpeggiator Helpers

`playhead_t` can run in a simple step-sequencer mode, but it also has a built-in arpeggiator mode. In arp mode, the playhead
loops over a set of active "strings", optionally repeating that set across several octaves. `setup_for_arp(...)` captures which
strings are active, how many octaves to span, and how many playhead steps are needed. Then `get_arp_string_idx()`,
`get_arp_string_mask()`, and `get_arp_octave()` tell you what the current step means musically.

`get_touched_string_mask(...)` is the normal way to derive the active-string set from a touched region of the grid.
*/
#define PLAY_SURFACE_PRESSURE_NOTE_ON 24
#define PLAY_SURFACE_PRESSURE_HOLD 16
#define PLAY_SURFACE_PRESSURE_EMA_FULL_SPEED 127
uint16_t get_touched_string_mask(int x, int y, int w, int h, int flags,
                                 int min_pressure_for_note_on = PLAY_SURFACE_PRESSURE_NOTE_ON);

struct playhead_t {
  static constexpr int MAX_STEPS = 1024;
  uint16_t position;             // what step we're on
  uint16_t from;                 // the step range
  uint16_t to;                   // inclusive!  so 0-511 is a full 512 steps; 0-15 is 16 steps etc.
  uint8_t num_strings;           // arp mode: how many active strings are being cycled through.
  uint8_t num_octaves;           // arp mode: how many octave repetitions are included in the loop.
  uint8_t shuffle_divide_shift;  // 0 = shuffle individual steps. 3 = shuffle 8-step blocks, then walk forward inside each block.
  bool looping;                  // false = stop once we reach the loop end
  bool gone_past_end;            // set once a non-looping playhead has reached the loop end
  bool on_first_step;            // set when we are sitting on the first step of the sequence
  bool on_last_step;             // same for last step.
  uint32_t skip_steps[MAX_STEPS / 32]; // a bitmask of steps we're to skip. leave all 0s for normal behavior.
  playorder_t order;
  uint32_t shuffle_seed; // change this to change the shuffle order, which is preserved on loop.
  int update(int num_steps, int requested_order = -1, int requested_pos = -1,
             bool ignore_transport = false); // returns how many times we arrive at the first step of a loop.
  // Plain reset: clears runtime latch state and does not eat a future divider edge.
  void reset(int requested_order = -1, int requested_pos = -1);
  // Rare cue/seek helper: call after reset/positioning when the next real divider edge should leave this position in place.
  void suppress_next_edge();
  inline int get_position_with_clock_divider(const clock_divider_t &clock_divider) const {
    return ((int)position << 8) + (clock_divider.phase >> 8);
  }

  /* Sets this up for a Plinky-style arpeggio.
  You provide the number of strings, which ones are active, and how many octaves to iterate over.
  A good way to compute the string mask is with `get_touched_string_mask(...)`, which tells you
  which strings in a play surface are being touched, but you can set it up how you like.
  After setting up for arp, use the playhead as usual, then call `get_arp_octave(...)`
  and `get_arp_string_idx(...)` (or mask) to find out which string is playing in the arp. */
  void setup_for_arp(int _num_strings, uint16_t string_mask, int num_octaves = 1);

  inline int get_arp_octave(void) const { return num_strings ? (position / num_strings) - ((num_octaves - 1) / 2) : 0; }
  inline int get_arp_string_idx(void) const { return num_strings ? position % num_strings : 0; }
  inline uint16_t get_arp_string_mask(void) const { return num_strings ? (1 << (position % num_strings)) & ~skip_steps[0] : 0; }

  // internal state
  uint32_t _valid_shuffle_steps[MAX_STEPS / 32];
  uint32_t _shuffle_seed_cur;
  bool _pingpong_backwards;
  bool _shuffle_ready;
  bool _suppress_step_after_reset;
  bool _update_up_to_single_step(bool is_step, int requested_order = -1, int requested_pos = -1, bool ignore_transport = false);
  void _take_step(void);
  int _reset_shuffle(void);
};

/*
## Semi-Immediate-Mode UI Helpers

The widget model is intentionally close to immediate-mode UI libraries: you call a widget while drawing the UI, and it reports
what just happened.

Simple controls like `button(...)` are fully immediate:

  `if (button(...)) { ... }`   // "pressed" edge

Controls that need a little retained interaction state, such as knobs, sliders, XY pads, and file pickers, use small helper
structs that you keep as members of your panel. Even then, the controls do not own the edited value: you pass the current
value in each frame, and if the widget reports activity you read `last_widget_*()` to find the new result.

Panels can also set one line of contextual help text with `set_help_text(...)`. That line is shown in the web second-screen view
and in the emulated views, and it supports lightweight inline markup for emphasis and colour. The same helper appears later in the
built-in synth section, but it is useful to think about it as part of the widget layer because it provides the "what should the
user do next?" text that accompanies the controls on screen.

Terminology used throughout this file:

  - pressed  = just went down
  - held/down = currently still held
  - released = just came up
*/
#define ISOLATED true
#define NOT_ISOLATED false

bool button(int x, int y, int colour, bool isolated = ISOLATED, const char *help_text = nullptr);
/* Same touch behaviour as `button(...)`, but does not draw or change the LED at `x,y`. */
bool invisible_button(int x, int y, bool isolated = ISOLATED, const char *help_text = nullptr);

/* `shift_button(...)` is the same as `button(...)`, except it returns true for as long as the pad is held,
not just on the press edge. */
bool shift_button(int x, int y, int colour, bool isolated = ISOLATED, const char *help_text = nullptr);
bool checkbox_button(int x, int y, int colour_on, int colour_off, bool *value, const char *help_text = nullptr);

/* radio_buttons draws a block of radio buttons with rainbow hues,
where the caller controls the hue and value range. It returns true if the user just changed the value.
use last_widget_value() to read the new value.
*/
bool radio_buttons(int x, int y, int w, int h, int hue_start, int hue_end, int val_start, int val_end, int cur_val,
                   bool light_unselected, const char *help_text);

enum {
  VALUE_INDICATOR_NONE,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_BRIGHTNESS,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_RADIUS,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_LEFT,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_RIGHT,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_UP,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_DOWN,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_HORIZONTAL,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_VERTICAL,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_BOTH,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_BIPOLAR_BRIGHTNESS,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_BIPOLAR_RADIUS,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_BIPOLAR_HORIZONTAL,
  VALUE_INDICATOR_BIPOLAR_VERTICAL,
};

void fill_rect_q8(int x1_q8, int y1_q8, int x2_q8, int y2_q8, uint32_t col);
void value_indicator_rect(int x, int y, int w, int h, int hue_start, int hue_end, int minval, int maxval, int value,
                          int style = VALUE_INDICATOR_NONE);
void value_indicator_circle(int x, int y, int w, int h, int hue_start, int hue_end, int minval, int maxval, int value,
                            int style = VALUE_INDICATOR_NONE);
/* Draws a one-pad-wide VU meter.
`peak` is a linear 0..32768 signal level. `HORIZONTAL` draws low-to-high from left to right; `VERTICAL` draws low-to-high
from `y + width - 1` up to `y`. `ratio_q8` is the threshold ratio between adjacent pads: 181 is
approximately 1/sqrt(2), or about 3 dB per pad. */
void draw_vu_meter(int x, int y, int width, int flags, int peak, int ratio_q8 = 181, uint32_t stem_col = GREEN);

/*
pressure button is a continuous pressure output. returns true if held or just released.
*/
bool pressure_button(int x, int y, uint32_t col, int pressure, int minval = 0, int maxval = 127, int pressure_smooth_amount = 1,
                     bool light_unselected = true, const char *help_text = nullptr);

/*
### Transport Button Widgets

These draw the conventional transport controls and update the shared transport/record state. They return true on the click edge.
The defaults match the usual bottom-right panel convention: play at `(15,15)`, stop at `(14,15)`, record at `(12,15)`.
*/
bool play_button(int x = 15, int y = 15, uint32_t col = GREEN, int midi_ports = -1);
bool stop_button(int x = 14, int y = 15, uint32_t col = RED, int midi_ports = -1);
bool rec_button(int x = 12, int y = 15, uint32_t col = RED);
bool tap_tempo_button(int x, int y, uint32_t col);


/* These functions refer to the most recently emitted widget in the current `on_ui(...)` pass. */
bool is_last_widget_held(void);     // returns true if the last ui button you emitted is being held down. (level)
bool is_last_widget_pressed(void);  // returns true if the last ui button you emitted is being pressed (edge)
bool is_last_widget_released(void); // returns true if the last ui button you emitted is being released (edge)

/* These report the microsecond timestamps for the current and previous `on_ui(...)` calls. */
uint32_t get_on_ui_time();       // time since boot for the current `on_ui(...)` call.
uint32_t get_last_on_ui_time();  // time since boot for the previous `on_ui(...)` call.
uint32_t get_on_ui_delta_time(); // elapsed microseconds between the current and previous `on_ui(...)` calls.

struct hold_timer_t {                    // helper for detecting buttons that are held for a bit.
  bool is_last_widget_held_for(int us) { // returns true once (an edge) when the button has been held for at least this long.
    update();
    if (!is_last_widget_held()) return false;
    int new_delta = get_on_ui_time() - start_time;
    int old_delta = get_last_on_ui_time() - start_time;
    return start_time && new_delta >= us && old_delta < us;
  }
  int last_widget_hold_time_us() {
    update();
    return (start_time && (is_last_widget_held() || is_last_widget_released())) ? (int)(get_on_ui_time() - start_time) : 0;
  } // returns how long the current button has been held for, in microseconds. returns 0 if not currently held.
  uint32_t start_time;
  void update() {
    if (is_last_widget_pressed()) start_time = get_on_ui_time();
  }
  int hold_time() {
    return start_time ? (int)(get_on_ui_time() - start_time) : 0;
  } // returns how long the current button has been held for, in microseconds. returns 0 if not currently held.
};
/*
### LED Transition Helpers

These helpers let a panel animate from the previous visible LED frame to the new one without keeping its own LED backup buffer.

Typical usage inside `on_ui(...)` is:

  1. decide that some event should start a transition
  2. before clearing the old frame, call `leds_backup()`
  3. call `leds_clear()` and render the new frame as normal
  4. at the end of `on_ui(...)`, call `leds_draw_transition_from_backup(...)` to mix the backed-up frame with the new frame

The backup buffer itself is global and always stores the full visible 16x16 LED frame. The timing is not stored here: panels keep
their own timer or phase and pass it in as `amount_q16`, where `0` means "show only the backed-up frame" and `65536` means "show
only the current frame".
*/
enum {
  LEDS_TRANSITION_CROSSFADE,
  LEDS_TRANSITION_FADE_VIA_BLACK,
  LEDS_TRANSITION_SCROLL_IN_FROM_LEFT,
  LEDS_TRANSITION_SCROLL_IN_FROM_TOP,
  LEDS_TRANSITION_SCROLL_IN_FROM_RIGHT,
  LEDS_TRANSITION_SCROLL_IN_FROM_BOTTOM,
};

void leds_backup(void); // capture the current visible 16x16 LED frame for later transition drawing.
void leds_draw_transition_from_backup(int x, int y, int w, int h, int transition, uint32_t amount_q16);
void leds_fade_rect_towards_color(int x, int y, int w, int h, uint32_t color,
                                  uint32_t amount_q16); // 0 does nothing; 65536 or above fills the region.

/*
### Touch Regions And Slider Flags

These are the lower-level building blocks used by sliders and custom controls. They expose the "where is the pressure and where
did it come from?" information needed for swipe-safe controls and position-aware widgets.

Touch handling is mostly pad-by-pad and stateless, but fingers can slide across the grid. There are three main tools to keep that
under control:

  - buttons can be `ISOLATED`, which rejects taps that are crowded by neighbouring touches
  - every active touch tracks its origin pad, so you can tell whether pressure arrived by sliding in from elsewhere
  - swipeable slider regions let related controls cooperate with each other while rejecting drags that started outside the region
*/
int count_touches_in_area(int x1, int y1, int x2, int y2,
                          bool previous = false); // if previous is true, it counts the touches in the area in the previous frame.

/* Gets the average pressure and position of all touches in the given rectangle.
`x` and `y` are returned with 8 bits of fractional position.
`x1`, `y1` is the top-left or bottom-left depending on the `from_bottom` flag.
`p` is the total pressure of all touches in the rectangle.
`minx`, `maxx` etc are the optional bounding box of any pads that are considered firmly touched.
It returns false if there are no firm touches anywhere in the rectangle.
Advanced: `ignore_pads_below` and `ignore_pads_above` describe the inclusive slice of pads to keep when calculating the pressure
and position. The slice is along y by default, or along x if `ignore_pads_along_x` is true. */
bool get_pressure_and_pos_in_rect(int x1, int y1, int w, int h, bool from_bottom, int *p, int *x, int *y, int *minx = NULL,
                                  int *miny = NULL, int *maxx = NULL, int *maxy = NULL, int ignore_pads_below = 0,
                                  int ignore_pads_above = 16, bool ignore_pads_along_x = false);

#define HORIZONTAL 1
#define VERTICAL 2

#define NO_STEM 0
#define SHOW_STEM 4
#define NEGATIVE_STEM 32 // a stem that goes down from maxvalue, instead of up from minvalue
#define CENTER_STEM 64   // a stem that goes from midvalue to minvalue and maxvalue to midvalue
#define ENABLE_CENTER_DETENT 128 // for CENTER_STEM, lock one-finger edits to one side of the midpoint

#define UNIPOLAR 0
#define BIPOLAR 8

#define PREFER_TO_KEEP_SINGLE_VALUE 16

#define RANDOM_SAME_COLOR 256 // for sliders, this sets the random part the same color as the main part.
#define RATCHET_RELATIVE 512  // for sliders, movement is relative to the previous finger position for finer edits.
#define SHOW_BACKGROUND 1024  // for sliders/knobs, add or floor a dim background behind the active control.
#define LOCK_SINGLE_VALUE 2048 // for range sliders, disable split editing and move both endpoints together.
#define READ_ONLY 4096         // for sliders, draw the control but ignore touches and report no edit.
#define FLAG_MOD_SRC_A 8192
#define FLAG_MOD_SRC_B 16384
#define FLAG_MOD_SRC_X 32768
#define FLAG_MOD_SRC_Y 65536
#define FLAG_MOD_SRC_PRESSURE 131072
#define FLAG_MOD_SRC_MASK (FLAG_MOD_SRC_A | FLAG_MOD_SRC_B | FLAG_MOD_SRC_X | FLAG_MOD_SRC_Y | FLAG_MOD_SRC_PRESSURE)
#define SYNTH_EDIT_CLEAR 262144
#define SYNTH_EDIT_REROLL 524288
#define SYNTH_EDIT_ACTION_MASK (SYNTH_EDIT_CLEAR | SYNTH_EDIT_REROLL)
#define SYNTH_SLIDER_EXTRA_PARAMS 1048576
static inline int mod_source_from_flags(int flags) {
  if (flags & FLAG_MOD_SRC_A) return MODULATION_SOURCE_A;
  if (flags & FLAG_MOD_SRC_B) return MODULATION_SOURCE_B;
  if (flags & FLAG_MOD_SRC_X) return MODULATION_SOURCE_X;
  if (flags & FLAG_MOD_SRC_Y) return MODULATION_SOURCE_Y;
  if (flags & FLAG_MOD_SRC_PRESSURE) return MODULATION_SOURCE_PRESSURE;
  return -1;
}
static inline int flags_for_mod_source(int mod_source) {
  switch (mod_source) {
  case MODULATION_SOURCE_A: return FLAG_MOD_SRC_A;
  case MODULATION_SOURCE_B: return FLAG_MOD_SRC_B;
  case MODULATION_SOURCE_X: return FLAG_MOD_SRC_X;
  case MODULATION_SOURCE_Y: return FLAG_MOD_SRC_Y;
  case MODULATION_SOURCE_PRESSURE: return FLAG_MOD_SRC_PRESSURE;
  default: return 0;
  }
}
static inline int synth_edit_action_from_flags(int flags) {
  if (flags & SYNTH_EDIT_CLEAR) return SYNTH_EDIT_CLEAR;
  if (flags & SYNTH_EDIT_REROLL) return SYNTH_EDIT_REROLL;
  return 0;
}
/*
Set or restore the global swipeable slider region.

Both setters return the previous region as a packed integer. Store that return value when a composite widget temporarily narrows
the swipeable area, then pass it to `set_swipeable_slider_region_packed(...)` before returning.
*/
uint32_t set_swipeable_slider_region(int x, int y, int w, int h);
uint32_t set_swipeable_slider_region_packed(uint32_t packed);
void clear_swipeable_slider_region(void);
bool in_swipeable_slider_region(int x, int y);

/* Returns true if both:
- the touch pad `x,y` is inside the swipeable slider region
- but the origin of that touch is outside

You can use this to create regions that resist being accidentally swiped into. */
bool touch_originates_outside_swipeable_slider_region(int x, int y);

bool touch_originates_inside_region(int padx, int pady, int x1, int y1, int w, int h);

/*
### Retained Widget State

Knobs, sliders, and XY pads need a small amount of private interaction state so they can keep drag gestures smooth across frames.
Keep these as members of your panel, then call the widget methods from `on_ui(...)`.
*/
struct knob2x2_t {
  uint8_t old_pressure;
  uint8_t state;
  int8_t old_touchx, old_touchy;
  int old_value;
  bool released;
  /* The ratio allows more turns of the real finger to be one turn of the knob.
  Higher ratio means finer-grained control. */
  int knob2x2(int x, int y, uint32_t col, int minval, int maxval, int value, const char *help_text = nullptr, int ratio = 1,
              int flags = 0);
  int large_knob(int x, int y, int w, int h, uint32_t col, uint32_t bg_col, int minval, int maxval, int value,
                 const char *help_text = nullptr, int flags = 0);
};

struct slider_t {
  uint8_t old_pressure;
  uint8_t state;
  uint8_t clamp_mode; // for center-stem detents and bipolar half latching
  uint8_t old_top_pressure;
  uint8_t old_bot_pressure;
  int _cached_from;
  int _cached_to;
  int _drag_start_from;
  int _drag_start_to;
  int16_t old_touch_i_q8;
  bool released;
  /* Note that the `x,y` coordinate is for the top-left corner of the slider.
  Flags combine orientation and style bits such as `SHOW_STEM`, `CENTER_STEM`, `ENABLE_CENTER_DETENT`, and `BIPOLAR`. */
  int simple_slider(int x, int y, int length, int flags, uint32_t col, int minval, int maxval, int value,
                    const char *help_text = nullptr, uint32_t min_col = 0);
  /* Range sliders edit a low/high span rather than a single point value. */
  int range_slider(int x, int y, int length, int flags, uint32_t col, int minval, int maxval, int from, int to,
                   const char *help_text = nullptr, uint32_t random_col = 0);

  /* Higher-level helper: edit one synth parameter on one preset slot using the current synth parameter metadata.
  `preset_idx_write_copies_mask` allows you to write to multiple presets at once. */
  int synth_param_slider(int x, int y, int length, int preset_idx, int synth_param, uint32_t col = 0, int flags = VERTICAL,
                         int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0);
};

/* Convenience helper for drawing a row or column of synth parameter sliders that all target the same preset slot.
`preset_idx_write_copies_mask` allows you to write to multiple presets at once.
Returns the index of the first slider that was touched. */
inline int synth_param_sliders_block(slider_t *synth_sliders, int x, int y, int width, int length, int preset_idx,
                                     const int *synth_params, int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0, int slider_flags = 0) {
  int rv = -1;
  for (int i = 0; i < width; ++i) {
    if (synth_sliders[i].synth_param_slider(x + i, y, length, preset_idx, synth_params[i], 0, VERTICAL | slider_flags,
                                            preset_idx_write_copies_mask))
      rv = synth_params[i];
  }
  return rv;
}

/* UI-only state for panels that expose modulation editing.
The recorded LFOs live on panel_t and should be serialised by the panel. A/B/X/Y are global sources; Pressure is combined
with each voice's pressure in the synth DSP path. */
struct mod_source_ui_state_t {
  static constexpr int NONE = -1;

  int8_t active_mod_source = NONE;
  int8_t button_chord_start_mod_source = NONE;
  slider_t lfo_slider_control = {};
  bool play_pause_consumed = false;
  bool play_pause_held = false;
  bool play_pause_released = false;

  void reset(void) {
    active_mod_source = NONE;
    button_chord_start_mod_source = NONE;
    lfo_slider_control = {};
    play_pause_consumed = false;
    play_pause_held = false;
    play_pause_released = false;
  }

  int flags(void) const { return flags_for_mod_source(active_mod_source); }

  void toggle(int mod_source, int x, int y) {
    if (active_mod_source == mod_source || mod_source == NONE) {
      active_mod_source = NONE;
      show_warp_save(8, 8, WHITE);
      return;
    }
    active_mod_source = (int8_t)mod_source;
    show_warp_load_mod_source(x, y, mod_source);
  }

  bool on_click(uint8_t button_mask) {
    uint8_t down = buttons_down();
    uint8_t clicked = buttons_clicked();
    if (clicked && (down & (uint8_t)~clicked) == 0) button_chord_start_mod_source = active_mod_source;
    uint8_t top_pair = BTN_BIT_LUP | BTN_BIT_RUP;
    uint8_t bottom_pair = BTN_BIT_LDOWN | BTN_BIT_RDOWN;
    if ((down & top_pair) == top_pair && (button_mask & top_pair) &&
        (button_clicked(BUTTON_LUP) || button_clicked(BUTTON_RUP))) {
      toggle(button_chord_start_mod_source == MODULATION_SOURCE_PRESSURE ? NONE : MODULATION_SOURCE_PRESSURE, 8, 0);
      return true;
    }
    if ((down & bottom_pair) == bottom_pair && (button_mask & bottom_pair) &&
        (button_clicked(BUTTON_LDOWN) || button_clicked(BUTTON_RDOWN))) {
      toggle(button_chord_start_mod_source == MODULATION_SOURCE_PRESSURE ? NONE : MODULATION_SOURCE_PRESSURE, 8, 15);
      return true;
    }
    if ((down & (down - 1)) != 0) return false;

    if ((button_mask & BTN_BIT_LUP) && button_clicked(BUTTON_LUP)) {
      toggle(MODULATION_SOURCE_A, 0, 0);
      return true;
    }
    if ((button_mask & BTN_BIT_RUP) && button_clicked(BUTTON_RUP)) {
      toggle(MODULATION_SOURCE_B, 15, 0);
      return true;
    }
    if ((button_mask & BTN_BIT_LDOWN) && button_clicked(BUTTON_LDOWN)) {
      toggle(MODULATION_SOURCE_X, 0, 15);
      return true;
    }
    if ((button_mask & BTN_BIT_RDOWN) && button_clicked(BUTTON_RDOWN)) {
      toggle(MODULATION_SOURCE_Y, 15, 15);
      return true;
    }
    return false;
  }

  bool on_click(uint8_t button_mask, bool mod_source_clicks_enabled) {
    if (!mod_source_clicks_enabled) {
      default_panel_button_click(button_mask);
      return false;
    }
    return on_click(button_mask);
  }

  bool lfo_slider(int x, int y, int width, int edit_flags = 0);
  bool lfo_slower_button(int x, int y);
  bool lfo_faster_button(int x, int y);
  bool lfo_play_pause_button(int x, int y, int edit_flags = 0, int fx_x = 0, int fx_y = 0, int fx_w = 0, int fx_h = 0);
  bool lfo_value_slider(int x, int y, int length, int slider_flags = HORIZONTAL | SHOW_STEM | CENTER_STEM,
                        int edit_flags = 0);
  void lfo_finish_frame(void);
};

/*
### `synth_flags_button`
this function is a helper to provide a button for one of the slice flags you can set using sounded.
there are 8 slices in a preset, and they can each have different flags. in practice they are often all the same,
and many presets only use 1 slice. you can specify a range of slices optionally; pressing a button sets all
slices in the range to the same value.

the 5 buttons are intended to be labelled: SIMPLE, TUNE, CHOP, LOOP, SYNC.
pass the enum as the second parameter.
the first 3 form a radio button betwene the 3 different 'tuning' modes.
* simple just pitches the sample up and down with the chosen key.
* tune adjusts the sample pitch on the fly to force its fundamental to the chosen key. this is based on pitch detection,
  so it can do odd things with unpitched sounds like drums.
* chop switches off keytracking and then sets the playback start point automatically across equal pieces of the sample,
  starting from the natural pitch (typically C4). the default chop count is 16.

the 'loop' button cycles between: one shot (black), loop (white), xfade loop second half (green), xfade last quarter (blue)
the 'sync' button toggles whether the sample is timestretched to match the BPM. this assumes that the sample is a loop
made up of 2, 4, 8 or 16 beats. it never stretches the sample more than 50% or 200% compared to its pitch.
*/
enum {
  SYNTH_FLAG_BUTTON_SIMPLE,
  SYNTH_FLAG_BUTTON_TUNE,
  SYNTH_FLAG_BUTTON_CHOP,
  SYNTH_FLAG_BUTTON_LOOP,
  SYNTH_FLAG_BUTTON_SYNC,
  SYNTH_FLAG_BUTTON_LOWPASS_GATE,
  SYNTH_FLAG_BUTTON_TUNINGMODE,
};

int synth_flags_button(int x, int y, int preset_idx, int synth_flag_enum, int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0, int col = 0,
                       int first_slice_idx = 0, int last_slice_idx = PRESET_SLICE_COUNT);

/*
### `xy_pad_t`

`xy_pad_t` is the retained-state helper for XY interaction.

It supports both a generic XY pad (`xy_pad`) and the higher-level synth XY pad (`synth_xy_pad`) that edits the preset XY position
for one channel. It also includes a small play-button helper so a synth pad can optionally double as a playable performance
surface.
*/
struct xy_pad_t : public slider_t {
  uint32_t play_button_click_time[XY_RECORDING_LAST];
  bool was_playing[XY_RECORDING_LAST];
  uint8_t prev_xy_pressure;
  uint8_t cur_xy_pressure;
  uint8_t last_touched_play_button;
  bool pad_used[XY_RECORDING_LAST]; // if xy was not used and you tap play, then we toggle playing
  /* Basic XY pad. */
  int xy_pad(int x, int y, int width, int height, uint32_t col, uint32_t bg_col, int minval, int maxval, int xval,
             int yval, const char *help_text = nullptr, bool read_only = false);

  /* Synth XY pad. The XY value of the pad is stored in the targeted preset slot.
  `preset_idx_write_copies_mask` allows you to write to multiple presets at once.
  `flags` may include `SYNTH_EDIT_CLEAR` or `SYNTH_EDIT_REROLL` for consistent panel-level edit actions. */
  int synth_xy_pad(int x, int y, int width, int height, int preset_idx, uint32_t col = WHITE, uint32_t bg_col = BLUE,
                   int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0, bool read_only = false, int flags = 0);
  bool lfo_button(int x, int y, int preset_idx, int recording_idx = XY_RECORDING_LFO, bool is_clear = false,
                  int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0, bool read_only = false, int flags = 0,
                  int fx_x = 0, int fx_y = 0, int fx_w = 0, int fx_h = 0);
  bool lfo_speed_control(int x, int y, int preset_idx, bool is_clear = false, int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0,
                         bool require_record_button_down = false, bool read_only = false);
};

#define XY_BUTTONS_ON_RIGHT false
#define XY_BUTTONS_ON_LEFT true

#define MAX_VOICES 12

/*
### `synth_xy_block(...)`

`synth_xy_block(...)` is the recommended way to add an all-in-one synth XY editor to your panel.

It combines:

  - a synth XY pad
  - an XY LFO record / play button
  - an XY Env record / play button for voice presets
  - the shared XY speed control

The helper also handles the subtle swipe/record interaction around the pad, so panels should generally prefer this routine over
manually placing `synth_xy_pad(...)`, `lfo_button(...)`, and `lfo_speed_control(...)`.

The `x, y` position is the top-left corner of the whole block, including the button column. By default the buttons are on the
right of the pad, but `buttons_on_left = true` flips the layout for panels that want the button column on the left.
*/
static inline bool synth_xy_block(xy_pad_t *xy_pad, int preset_idx, int x, int y, int width = 7, int height = 5,
                                  uint32_t col = WHITE, uint32_t bg_col = BLUE, bool is_clear = false,
                                  int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0, bool buttons_on_left = XY_BUTTONS_ON_RIGHT,
                                  bool require_record_button_down_for_speed = false, bool read_only = false, int flags = 0) {
  if (!xy_pad) return false;
  if (is_clear) flags |= SYNTH_EDIT_CLEAR;
  int pad_x = buttons_on_left ? (x + 1) : x;
  int button_x = buttons_on_left ? x : (x + width);
  bool mod_mode = mod_source_from_flags(flags) >= 0;
  int edit_action = synth_edit_action_from_flags(flags);
  int control_flags = mod_mode ? (flags & ~SYNTH_EDIT_ACTION_MASK) : flags;
  bool clear_action = !mod_mode && edit_action == SYNTH_EDIT_CLEAR;
  bool controls_read_only = read_only || mod_mode;
  /* This sets a swipeable region that covers the XY pad and the buttons next to it.
  It prevents a finger held down on the record button from confusing swipe detection when it is right next to the XY pad.
  The bug is subtle, which is why this helper is recommended over rolling your own. */
  uint32_t old_sr = set_swipeable_slider_region(x, y, width + 1, height);
  if (height >= 1)
    xy_pad->lfo_button(button_x, y + 0, preset_idx, XY_RECORDING_LFO, clear_action, preset_idx_write_copies_mask,
                       controls_read_only, control_flags, pad_x, y, width, height);
  if (height >= 2 && preset_idx != MIX_PRESET_IDX) {
    xy_pad->lfo_button(button_x, y + 1, preset_idx, XY_RECORDING_ENV, clear_action, preset_idx_write_copies_mask,
                       controls_read_only, control_flags, pad_x, y, width, height);
  }
  if (height >= 4)
    xy_pad->lfo_speed_control(button_x, y + height - 2, preset_idx, clear_action, preset_idx_write_copies_mask,
                              require_record_button_down_for_speed,
                              controls_read_only || (!mod_mode && edit_action == SYNTH_EDIT_REROLL));
  bool rv = xy_pad->synth_xy_pad(pad_x, y, width, height, preset_idx, col, bg_col, preset_idx_write_copies_mask,
                                 read_only, flags);
  set_swipeable_slider_region_packed(old_sr);
  return rv;
}

/* Return the current XY value of the XY pad for the given preset.
If you include playback, it also includes the animating LFO value. */
void get_synth_xy_pad_pos(int preset_idx, int *x, int *y, bool include_playback);
/* Apply a shared synth edit action without drawing a widget.
Pass `SYNTH_EDIT_CLEAR` or `SYNTH_EDIT_REROLL`; optional copy masks use the same semantics as the widget helpers. */
bool synth_apply_param_edit_action(int preset_idx, int synth_param, int edit_action, int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0);
bool synth_apply_xy_edit_action(int preset_idx, int edit_action, int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0);
bool synth_apply_xy_recording_edit_action(int preset_idx, int recording_idx, int edit_action,
                                          int preset_idx_write_copies_mask = 0);
/* Return quadrant, clockwise starting with top-left.
`x, y   0,FF  FF,FF  -> 0 1`
`       0,0   FF,0      3 2`
If `include_center`, the exact midpoint returns 4.
`is_exact_corner` returns whether the pad is at one of the extreme coordinates, including center. */
uint8_t get_quadrant_xy_pad_pos(int preset_idx, bool include_center, bool *is_exact_corner);
int get_mod_src_lfo_value_q14(int mod_source, bool include_hardware_input);

int last_widget_new_from(void);
int last_widget_new_to(void);
int last_widget_new_value(void);
void set_last_widget_new_from(int value);
void set_last_widget_new_to(int value);
void set_last_widget_new_from_to(int from, int to);
void set_last_widget_new_value(int value);
#define last_file_picker_new_value last_widget_new_value

uint32_t progressive_color(int value, uint32_t colour); // from off (<0) to dimmest (0) to brightest (3) to flashing (>3)

/*
### `voice_allocator_t`

`voice_allocator_t` is a small shared helper for panels that need several note sources to coexist on the same pool of 12 synth
voices.

The panel provides a deterministic 32-bit `source_id` for each logical note source. The allocator owns the mapping from that
`source_id` to a synth voice. This means panels do not store allocator-owned handles; instead they just reconstruct the same
`source_id` each `on_sequence(...)` or midi event.

`voice_allocate(...)` uses `sequence_frame_counter` as a per-`on_sequence(...)` stamp. A voice claimed this frame is protected
from later equal-priority claims in the same frame, which makes over-contended cases much more stable as long as the panel submits
claims in a consistent order. A request with `prio == 0` releases the existing voice for that `source_id`, but the inactive slot
keeps that id as a hint so a later matching claim can reuse the same physical voice if nothing else has taken it.

`garbage_collect_voices(...)` is a small safety-net helper for panels using the allocator in an immediate-mode style. Voice age is
measured in `on_sequence(...)` frames from `sequence_frame_counter`, so age `0` means "claimed this frame". The common case is
`garbage_collect_voices(1)`, which releases any allocator voice that was not refreshed this frame.

Typical usage:

  - `uint32_t source_id = ...;`
  - `int old_voice = voice_allocator->find_voice(source_id);`
  - `int new_voice = voice_allocator->voice_allocate(source_id, prio);`
  - `if (new_voice != old_voice) synth_note_up(old_voice);`
  - `play_synth(new_voice, preset_idx, velocity, note_q8, retrigger);`

Monophonic vs polyphonic behaviour is deliberately not part of the allocator API. Panels express that by how they construct
`source_id`: reuse one id to keep reusing one voice, or generate a fresh id when they want a fresh voice.
*/
struct voice_alloc_state_t {
  uint32_t source_id;
  uint32_t seq;
  uint8_t prio;
};
struct voice_allocator_t {
  voice_alloc_state_t _state[MAX_VOICES];

  int voice_allocate(uint32_t source_id, uint8_t prio, uint8_t subset_start = 0, uint8_t subset_end = MAX_VOICES);
  inline int voice_deallocate(uint32_t source_id, uint8_t subset_start = 0, uint8_t subset_end = MAX_VOICES) {
    return voice_allocate(source_id, 0, subset_start, subset_end);
  }
  uint16_t garbage_collect_voices(uint32_t min_age = 1, uint32_t source_id_mask = 0, uint32_t source_id_value = 0,
                                  uint8_t subset_start = 0, uint8_t subset_end = MAX_VOICES);
  int find_voice(uint32_t source_id, uint8_t subset_start = 0, uint8_t subset_end = MAX_VOICES) const;
};

/*
### `finger_tracker_t`

`finger_tracker_t` is a small raw-touch helper for panels that want stable finger ids over a rectangular pad region.

The tracked state is intentionally open and SOA-style: `finger_x[]`/`finger_y[]` are absolute pad coordinates in q8,
`finger_p[]` is the total pressure in the tracked patch, `finger_frames[]` counts how many update frames the finger has
survived, `finger_down_time_us[]` stores the creation timestamp in microseconds, and `active_mask` marks live slots.
Down timestamps remain readable after release until that slot is reused, so release handlers can query hold duration.
`finger_id[]` is a stable nonzero id for live slots.
*/
struct finger_tracker_t {
  enum { MAX_FINGERS = 8 };

  int16_t finger_x[MAX_FINGERS];
  int16_t finger_y[MAX_FINGERS];
  int16_t finger_p[MAX_FINGERS];
  uint16_t finger_frames[MAX_FINGERS];
  uint32_t finger_down_time_us[MAX_FINGERS];
  uint8_t finger_id[MAX_FINGERS];
  uint8_t active_mask;
  uint8_t next_finger_id;

  void clear(void) { memset(this, 0, sizeof(*this)); }

  bool active(int idx) const { return idx >= 0 && idx < MAX_FINGERS && ((active_mask & (uint8_t)(1u << idx)) != 0); }

  bool is_new(int idx) const { return active(idx) && finger_frames[idx] == 1; }

  bool has_down_time(int idx) const { return idx >= 0 && idx < MAX_FINGERS && finger_down_time_us[idx] != 0; }

  uint32_t held_us(int idx, uint32_t now) const {
    return has_down_time(idx) ? (uint32_t)(now - finger_down_time_us[idx]) : 0;
  }

  bool held_less_than_us(int idx, uint32_t now, uint32_t limit_us) const {
    return has_down_time(idx) && held_us(idx, now) < limit_us;
  }

  bool held_at_least_us(int idx, uint32_t now, uint32_t limit_us) const {
    return has_down_time(idx) && held_us(idx, now) >= limit_us;
  }

  int count_active(void) const { return __builtin_popcount((unsigned)active_mask); }

  int update(int x0, int y0, int w, int h, const uint16_t *blocked_pads = nullptr);

  void draw_finger(int idx, uint32_t col) const;
};

/*
### `play_surface_t`

`play_surface_t` turns a rectangular touch region into a playable note surface.

It tracks up to 16 active fingers, remembers which voice each finger owns, and can interpret the same touched region in several
ways: whole-surface monophonic mode, independent-pad polyphonic mode, original-Plinky one-note-per-string mode, or multi-touch
per-string mode. It is a good fit when you want touch input to behave like an instrument rather than like a collection of
independent buttons.

Internally it uses a compact `finger_t` record for each active contact, which stores the tracked string, pressure, and the voice
currently assigned to that touch.
*/
/* This is the state used by the play surface to track fingers and map them to voices. */
struct finger_t {
  uint16_t string_pos_q8 : 12; // 0-15*256
  uint16_t string_idx : 4;     // 0-15
  uint8_t pressure;            // 0-255
  uint8_t voice_idx : 6;       // 63 means 'no voice'
  uint8_t is_new : 1;          // 1 = freshly allocated. (ie retrigger)
  uint8_t is_touch : 1;        // 1 = came from a touch; 0 = came from a virtual finger directly poked.
  inline int16_t fretted() const {
    return (string_pos_q8 + 128) >> 8;
  } // round pos to the nearest 'fret', and return that fret index.
  inline int16_t fretted_q8() const {
    return (string_pos_q8 + 128) & (~255);
  } // round pos to the nearest 'fret', but keep it 8 bit fractional.
  inline int16_t fretless_q8() const { return string_pos_q8; }
};
static_assert(sizeof(finger_t) == 4, "finger_t must be 4 bytes");

#define PLAY_SURFACE_MODE_MASK 12
#define TRACK_FINGERS_ACROSS_STRINGS 16
#define POLYPHONIC 0 // every pad is independent; no fractional in-between positions.
#define MONOPHONIC 4 // one touch across the whole surface; highest-priority string wins.
#define STRINGOPHONIC_MONO 8 // one touch per string, but polyphonic across strings.
#define STRINGOPHONIC_ORIGINAL_PLINKY_STYLE STRINGOPHONIC_MONO
#define STRINGOPHONIC_POLY 12 // multiple touches per string, using peak finding along the string direction.

typedef void (*play_surface_note_fn)(void *user, int voice, int note, uint8_t velocity, finger_t finger);
typedef uint8_t (*scale_play_surface_brightness_fn)(void *user, int string_idx, int string_pos, int x, int y, int note);

int scale_play_surface_note(int string_root_note, int string_pos, int scale_root_note, uint16_t scale);

static inline void fill_scale_string_roots(uint8_t *string_roots, int string_count, int starting_pitch, uint16_t scale_mask,
                                           int interval_degrees) {
  if (!string_roots || string_count <= 0) return;
  scale_t scale = scale_from_bitmask(scale_mask);
  for (int i = 0; i < string_count; ++i) {
    string_roots[i] = (uint8_t)clampi(shift_note_along_scale(i * interval_degrees, starting_pitch, starting_pitch, scale), 0, 127);
  }
}

struct play_surface_t {
  finger_t fingers[16];
  uint32_t finger_seq[16];
  uint32_t next_finger_seq;
  uint32_t get_finger_age(int finger_idx) {
    return (finger_idx >= 0 && finger_idx < 16) ? (next_finger_seq - finger_seq[finger_idx]) : 0;
  }
  /* `update_from_touch(...)` updates the play surface from the touch surface in the given region.
  It returns the number of fingers that are down right now.

  `x1,y1` is the top-left corner of the source rectangle. This stays true in both `HORIZONTAL`
  and `VERTICAL` modes; vertical mode only changes how touches inside that rectangle map onto
  strings and fractional positions.

  Geometry and polyphony style are separate:
  - `HORIZONTAL` / `VERTICAL` choose which axis is the string direction, and callers should pass one explicitly
  - `POLYPHONIC` means every pad is its own touch target, with no fractional in-between positions
